	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Mormonism</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-fr-revisiontag" class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><span aria-disabled='false' title='Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page.' class='flaggedrevs-icon oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-iconElement-icon oo-ui-icon-articleSearch oo-ui-iconElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-iconWidget'></span><span id='mw-fr-revisiontoggle' aria-disabled='false' title='show/hide details' class='fr-toggle-arrow oo-ui-widget oo-ui-widget-enabled oo-ui-indicatorElement-indicator oo-ui-indicator-down oo-ui-indicatorElement oo-ui-labelElement-invisible oo-ui-indicatorWidget'></span></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" class="flaggedrevs_short_details" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Mormonism">reviewed</a> on <i>21 September 2019</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the Mormon religion. For the largest and most prominent branch of Mormonism, see <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a>. For the people known as Mormons, see <a href="/wiki/Mormons" title="Mormons">Mormons</a>. For the general religious movement, see <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saint movement</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Mormon_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Mormon (disambiguation)">Mormon (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Religious tradition of the Latter Day Saint movement</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mormon-book.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mormon-book.jpg/220px-Mormon-book.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mormon-book.jpg/330px-Mormon-book.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Mormon-book.jpg/440px-Mormon-book.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="863" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mormon-book.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a></div></div></div>
<p><b>Mormonism</b> is the predominant religious tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saint movement</a> of <a href="/wiki/Restorationism" title="Restorationism">Restorationist</a> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> started by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_New_York" title="Western New York">Western New York</a> in the 1820s and 30s.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mormon_(word)" title="Mormon (word)">word <i>Mormon</i></a> originally derived from the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a>, a religious text published by Smith, which he said he translated from <a href="/wiki/Golden_plates" title="Golden plates">golden plates</a> with divine assistance. The book describes itself as a chronicle of early <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> and their dealings with <a href="/wiki/God_in_Mormonism" title="God in Mormonism">God</a>. Based on the book's name, Smith's early followers were more widely known as <i>Mormons</i>, and their faith <i>Mormonism</i>. The term was initially considered pejorative,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> but Mormons no longer consider it so (although generally preferring other terms such as Latter-day Saint or LDS).<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Joseph_Smith" title="Death of Joseph Smith">Smith was killed</a> in 1844, most Mormons followed <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young" title="Brigham Young">Brigham Young</a> on his westward journey to the area that became the <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">Utah Territory</a>, calling themselves <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (LDS Church). Other sects include <a href="/wiki/Mormon_fundamentalism" title="Mormon fundamentalism">Mormon fundamentalism</a>, which seeks to maintain practices and doctrines such as <a href="/wiki/Plural_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Plural marriage">polygamy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and other small independent denominations. The second-largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_denominations_in_the_Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="List of denominations in the Latter Day Saint movement">Latter Day Saint denomination</a>, the Reorganized Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints, since 2001 called the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Christ" title="Community of Christ">Community of Christ</a>, does not describe itself as "Mormon", but follows a <a href="/wiki/Trinitarianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinitarianism">Trinitarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Restorationism_(Christian_primitivism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Restorationism (Christian primitivism)">Christian restorationist</a> theology, and considers itself <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Latter-day_Saints)" class="mw-redirect" title="Restoration (Latter-day Saints)">Restorationist</a> in terms of Latter Day Saint doctrine.
</p><p>Mormonism has common beliefs with the rest of the Latter Day Saint movement, including the use of and belief in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>, and in other religious texts including the Book of Mormon and <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_and_Covenants" title="Doctrine and Covenants">Doctrine and Covenants</a>. It also accepts the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_of_Great_Price_(Mormonism)" title="Pearl of Great Price (Mormonism)">Pearl of Great Price</a> as part of its scriptural canon, and has a history of teaching <a href="/wiki/Celestial_marriage" title="Celestial marriage">eternal marriage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exaltation_(LDS_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Exaltation (LDS Church)">eternal progression</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plural_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Plural marriage">polygamy (plural marriage)</a>, although the LDS Church formally abandoned the practice of plural marriage in 1890. <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="Culture of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">Cultural Mormonism</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyle</a> promoted by Mormon institutions, includes <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Mormon" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural Mormon">cultural Mormons</a> who identify with the culture, but not necessarily the theology.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Brief_history">Brief history</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="History of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">History of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg/170px-Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg/255px-Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg/340px-Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg 2x" data-file-width="381" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Joseph_Smith_first_vision_stained_glass.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A stained glass window showing <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a>'s <a href="/wiki/First_Vision" title="First Vision">First Vision</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Mormonism originated in the 1820s in western <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> during a period of religious excitement known as the <a href="/wiki/Second_Great_Awakening" title="Second Great Awakening">Second Great Awakening</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> After praying about which <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">denomination</a> he should join, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith, Jr.</a> said he received a vision in the spring of 1820.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Called the "<a href="/wiki/First_Vision" title="First Vision">First Vision</a>", Smith said <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">God the Father</a> instructed him to join none of the existing churches because they were all wrong.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> During the 1820s Smith reported several angelic visitations, and was eventually told that God would use him to re-establish the true Christian church, and that the Book of Mormon would be the means of establishing correct doctrine for the restored church. Smith, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cowdery" title="Oliver Cowdery">Oliver Cowdery</a>, and other early followers, began <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptizing</a> new converts in 1829. Formally organized in 1830 as the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Christ_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Church of Christ (Latter Day Saints)">Church of Christ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Smith was seen by his followers as a modern-day <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Joseph Smith said the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a> was translated from writing on <a href="/wiki/Golden_plates" title="Golden plates">golden plates</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Egyptian" title="Reformed Egyptian">reformed Egyptian</a> language, translated with the assistance of the <a href="/wiki/Urim_and_Thummim" title="Urim and Thummim">Urim and Thummim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seer_stone_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Seer stone (Latter Day Saints)">seer stones</a>.  Both the special spectacles and the seer stone were at times referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Urim_and_Thummim_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Urim and Thummim (Latter Day Saints)">Urim and Thummim</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-ldstranslation_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ldstranslation-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He said an <a href="/wiki/Angel_Moroni" title="Angel Moroni">angel</a> first showed him the location of the plates in 1823, buried in a <a href="/wiki/Cumorah" title="Cumorah">nearby hill</a>, but he was not allowed to take the plates until 1827. Smith began dictating the text of <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Mormon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Book of Mormon">The Book of Mormon</a> around the fall of 1827 until the summer of 1828 when <a href="/wiki/Lost_116_pages" title="Lost 116 pages">116 pages were lost</a>. Translation began again in April 1829 and finished in June 1829,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> saying that he translated it "by the <a href="/wiki/Gifts_of_the_Spirit_in_Mormonism" title="Gifts of the Spirit in Mormonism">gift and power of God</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Oliver Cowdery acted as scribe for the majority of the translation.  After the translation was completed, Smith said the plates were returned to the angel.  During Smith's supposed possession, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon_witnesses" title="Book of Mormon witnesses">very few people were allowed to "witness" the plates</a>.
</p><p>The book described itself as a chronicle of an early <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">Israelite diaspora</a>, integrating with the pre-existing <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">indigenous peoples of the Americas</a>, written by a people called the <a href="/wiki/Nephite" class="mw-redirect" title="Nephite">Nephites</a>. According to <a href="/wiki/The_Book_of_Mormon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Book of Mormon">The Book of Mormon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lehi_(Book_of_Mormon_prophet)" title="Lehi (Book of Mormon prophet)">Lehi's</a> family left <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> at the urging of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Mormonism" title="God in Mormonism">God</a> c. 600 BC, and later sailed to the Americas c. 589 BC. The Nephites are described as descendants of <a href="/wiki/Nephi,_son_of_Lehi" title="Nephi, son of Lehi">Nephi</a>, the fourth son of the prophet <a href="/wiki/Lehi_(Book_of_Mormon_prophet)" title="Lehi (Book of Mormon prophet)">Lehi</a>. The Nephites are portrayed as having a belief in <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Christ</a> hundreds of years before <a href="/wiki/Nativity_of_Jesus" title="Nativity of Jesus">his birth</a>. Historical accuracy and veracity of the Book of Mormon was and continues to be <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Book_of_Mormon" title="Criticism of the Book of Mormon">hotly contested</a>. No archaeological, linguistic, or other evidence of the use of Egyptian writing in ancient America has been discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To avoid confrontation with New York residents, the members moved to <a href="/wiki/Kirtland,_Ohio" title="Kirtland, Ohio">Kirtland, Ohio</a>, and hoped to establish a permanent <a href="/wiki/New_Jerusalem" title="New Jerusalem">New Jerusalem</a> or <a href="/wiki/Zion_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Zion (Latter Day Saints)">City of Zion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Missouri" title="Jackson County, Missouri">Jackson County, Missouri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> However, they were expelled from Jackson County in 1833 and fled to other parts of Missouri in 1838. <a href="/wiki/Mormon_War_(1838)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormon War (1838)">Violence</a> between the Missourians and church members resulted in the governor of Missouri issuing an "<a href="/wiki/Missouri_Executive_Order_44" title="Missouri Executive Order 44">extermination order</a>," again forcing the church to relocate.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The displaced Mormons fled to Illinois, to a small town called Commerce. The church bought the town, renamed it <a href="/wiki/Nauvoo,_Illinois" title="Nauvoo, Illinois">Nauvoo</a>, and lived with a degree of peace and prosperity for a few years.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> However, tensions between Mormons and non-Mormons again escalated, and in 1844 Smith was <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Joseph_Smith" title="Death of Joseph Smith">killed</a> by a mob, precipitating a <a href="/wiki/Succession_crisis_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Succession crisis (Latter Day Saints)">succession crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The largest group of Mormons (LDS Church) accepted <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young" title="Brigham Young">Brigham Young</a> as the new prophet/leader and emigrated to what became the <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">Utah Territory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> There, the church began the open practice of <a href="/wiki/Plural_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Plural marriage">plural marriage</a>, a form of <a href="/wiki/Polygyny" title="Polygyny">polygyny</a> which Smith had instituted in Nauvoo. Plural marriage became the faith's most sensational characteristic during the 19th century, but vigorous opposition by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> threatened the church's existence as a legal institution. Further, polygamy was also a major cause for the opposition to Mormonism in the states of Idaho and Arizona.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1890_Manifesto" title="1890 Manifesto">1890 Manifesto</a>, church president <a href="/wiki/Wilford_Woodruff" title="Wilford Woodruff">Wilford Woodruff</a> announced the official end of plural marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the formal abolition of plural marriage in 1890, several smaller groups of Mormons broke with the LDS Church forming several denominations of <a href="/wiki/Mormon_fundamentalism" title="Mormon fundamentalism">Mormon fundamentalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, the LDS Church had become a proponent of <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">patriotism</a>, has extended its reach internationally by a vigorous <a href="/wiki/Missionary_(LDS_Church)" title="Missionary (LDS Church)">missionary</a> program, and has grown in size to a reported membership of over 16 million.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The church is becoming a part of the American and international mainstream.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> However, it consciously and intentionally retains its identity as a "peculiar people,"<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> believing their unique relationship with God helps save them from <a href="/wiki/World" title="World">"worldliness"</a> (non-spiritual influences).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theology">Theology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_of_God">Nature of God</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/God_in_Mormonism" title="God in Mormonism">God in Mormonism</a></div>
<p>Like most other Christian groups, Mormonism teaches that there is <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">the Father</a>, <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">the Son</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_in_Christianity" title="Holy Spirit in Christianity">Holy Spirit</a>, but unlike <a href="/wiki/Trinitarianism" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinitarianism">trinitarian</a> faiths, the LDS Church teaches that they are <a href="/wiki/Nontrinitarianism" title="Nontrinitarianism">separate and distinct beings</a> with the Father and Son having perfected physical bodies and the Holy Ghost having only a body of spirit.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> While the three beings are physically distinct, in Mormon theology they are one in thoughts, actions, and purpose and commonly referred to collectively as the "Godhead".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Also, Mormonism teaches that God the Father is the literal father of the spirits of all men and women, which existed prior to their mortal existence.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The LDS Church also believes that a <a href="/wiki/Heavenly_Mother_(Mormonism)" title="Heavenly Mother (Mormonism)">Heavenly Mother</a> exists.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Further, it is believed that all humans as children of God can become <a href="/wiki/Exaltation_(Mormonism)" title="Exaltation (Mormonism)">exalted</a>, inheriting all that God has, as joint-heirs with Christ, and <a href="/wiki/Apotheosis#Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="Apotheosis">becoming like him as a God</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Snow" title="Lorenzo Snow">Lorenzo Snow</a> is quoted as saying "As man now is God once was: As God now is, man may be."<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration">Restoration</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg/180px-Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="253" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg/270px-Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg/360px-Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1806" data-file-height="2534" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Priesthood_Restoration_Crocheron.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A depiction of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cowdery" title="Oliver Cowdery">Oliver Cowdery</a> receiving <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Priesthood (Latter Day Saints)">priesthood</a> authority from <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Restoration (Latter Day Saints)">Restoration (Latter Day Saints)</a></div>
<p>Mormonism describes itself as falling within world <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, but as a distinct restored <a href="/wiki/Dispensation_(period)#Latter_Day_Saint_dispensations" title="Dispensation (period)">dispensation</a>; it characterizes itself as the only true form of the Christian religion since the time of a <a href="/wiki/Great_Apostasy_(Latter_Day_Saints)" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Apostasy (Latter Day Saints)">Great Apostasy</a> that began not long after the <a href="/wiki/Ascension_of_Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascension of Jesus Christ">ascension of Jesus Christ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PMG35_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PMG35-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> According to Mormons this Apostasy involved the corruption of the pure, original Christian doctrine with <a href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek philosophy">Greek</a> and other philosophies,<sup id="cite_ref-GA2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GA2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and followers dividing into different ideological groups.<sup id="cite_ref-MARV1_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MARV1-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
Additionally, Mormons claim the <a href="/wiki/Martyr" title="Martyr">martyrdom</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">Apostles</a>
led to the loss of <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Priesthood (Latter Day Saints)">Priesthood authority</a> to administer the Church and its ordinances.<sup id="cite_ref-GA1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GA1-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Eyring_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eyring-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mormons believe that God re-established the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">early Christian</a> Church as found in the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> through Joseph Smith.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In particular, Mormons believe that angels such as <a href="/wiki/Saint_Peter" title="Saint Peter">Peter</a>, <a href="/wiki/James,_son_of_Zebedee" class="mw-redirect" title="James, son of Zebedee">James</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">John</a>, and <a href="/wiki/John_the_Baptist" title="John the Baptist">John the Baptist</a> appeared to Joseph Smith and others and bestowed various <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Priesthood (Latter Day Saints)">Priesthood</a> authorities on them.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Mormons thus believe that their Church is the "only true and living church" because divine authority was restored to it through Smith. In addition, Mormons believe that Smith and his legitimate successors are modern prophets who receive revelation from God to guide the church. They maintain that other religions have a portion of the truth and are guided by the <a href="/wiki/Light_of_Christ_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Light of Christ (Latter Day Saints)">light of Christ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmology">Cosmology</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mormon_cosmology" title="Mormon cosmology">Mormon cosmology</a></div>
<p>Smith's cosmology is laid out mostly in Smith's later revelations and sermons, but particularly the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Abraham" title="Book of Abraham">Book of Abraham</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Moses" title="Book of Moses">Book of Moses</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/King_Follett_discourse" title="King Follett discourse">King Follett discourse</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mormon_cosmology" title="Mormon cosmology">Mormon cosmology</a> presents a unique view of God and the universe, and places a high importance on human agency. In Mormonism, life on earth is just a short part of an eternal existence. Mormons believe that in the beginning, all people existed as <a href="/wiki/Spirit" title="Spirit">spirits</a> or "intelligences," in the presence of God.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In this state, God proposed a <a href="/wiki/Plan_of_salvation_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Plan of salvation (Latter Day Saints)">plan of salvation</a> whereby they could progress and "have a privilege to advance like himself."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The spirits were free to accept or reject this plan, and a "third" of them, led by <a href="/wiki/Satan" title="Satan">Satan</a> rejected it.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The rest accepted the plan, coming to earth and receiving bodies with an understanding that they would experience sin and suffering.
</p><p>In Mormonism, the central part of God's plan is the <a href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Atonement in Christianity">atonement</a> of Jesus Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Mormons believe that one purpose of earthly life is to learn to choose good over evil. In this process, people inevitably make mistakes, becoming unworthy to return to the presence of God. Mormons believe that Jesus paid for the sins of the world and that all people can be saved through his atonement.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Mormons accept Christ's atonement through faith, repentance, formal covenants or ordinances such as baptism, and consistently trying to live a Christ-like life.
</p><p>According to Mormon scripture, the Earth's creation was not <i><a href="/wiki/Ex_nihilo" title="Ex nihilo">ex nihilo</a></i>, but organized from existing matter. The Earth is just one of many inhabited worlds, and there are many governing heavenly bodies, including the planet or star <a href="/wiki/Kolob" title="Kolob">Kolob</a>, which is said to be nearest the <a href="/wiki/Throne_of_God" title="Throne of God">throne of God</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ordinances">Ordinances</span></h3>
<p>In Mormonism, an <a href="/wiki/Ordinance_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Ordinance (Latter Day Saints)">ordinance</a> is a religious <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">ritual</a> of special significance, often involving the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Covenant (Latter Day Saints)">covenant</a> with God.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Ordinances are performed by the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Mormonism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Priesthood (Mormonism)">priesthood</a> and in the name of <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus Christ</a>. The term has a meaning roughly similar to that of the term "<a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a>" in other Christian denominations.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG/220px-LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG/330px-LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG/440px-LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2856" data-file-height="2142" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LDS_Baptism_Panama.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An eight-year-old girl being baptized in Cerro Punta, Panama</div></div></div>
<p>Saving ordinances (or ordinances viewed as necessary for salvation) include: <a href="/wiki/Baptism_(Latter_Day_Saints)" class="mw-redirect" title="Baptism (Latter Day Saints)">baptism</a> by <a href="/wiki/Immersion_baptism" title="Immersion baptism">immersion</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_accountability" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of accountability">age of accountability</a> (normally age 8); <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Confirmation (Latter Day Saints)">confirmation</a> and reception of the <a href="/wiki/Baptism_with_the_Holy_Spirit" title="Baptism with the Holy Spirit">gift of the Holy Ghost</a>, performed by laying hands on the head of a newly baptized member; ordination to the <a href="/wiki/Aaronic_priesthood_(LDS_Church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aaronic priesthood (LDS Church)">Aaronic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melchizedek_priesthood" class="mw-redirect" title="Melchizedek priesthood">Melchizedek priesthoods</a> for males; an <a href="/wiki/Endowment_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Endowment (Latter Day Saints)">endowment</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Washing_and_anointing" title="Washing and anointing">washing and anointing</a>) received in <a href="/wiki/Temple_(LDS_Church)" title="Temple (LDS Church)">temples</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Celestial_marriage" title="Celestial marriage">marriage</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Sealing_(Latter_Day_Saints)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sealing (Latter Day Saints)">sealing</a>) to a spouse.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mormons also perform other ordinances, which include the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_supper" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord&#39;s supper">Lord's supper</a> (commonly called the <a href="/wiki/Sacrament_(Latter_Day_Saints)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacrament (Latter Day Saints)">sacrament</a>), <a href="/wiki/Naming_and_blessing_children" class="mw-redirect" title="Naming and blessing children">naming and blessing children</a>, giving <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_blessing" title="Priesthood blessing">priesthood blessings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patriarchal_blessings" class="mw-redirect" title="Patriarchal blessings">patriarchal blessings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_Sick" class="mw-redirect" title="Anointing of the Sick">anointing and blessing the sick</a>, participating in <a href="/wiki/Prayer_circle_(Mormonism)" title="Prayer circle (Mormonism)">prayer circles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Setting_apart" title="Setting apart">setting apart</a> individuals who are called to church positions.
</p><p>In Mormonism, the saving ordinances are seen as necessary for salvation, but they are not sufficient in and of themselves. For example, baptism is required for <a href="/wiki/Exaltation_(Mormonism)" title="Exaltation (Mormonism)">exaltation</a>, but simply having been baptized does not guarantee any eternal reward. The baptized person is expected to be obedient to God's commandments, to repent of any sinful conduct subsequent to baptism, and to receive the other saving ordinances.
</p><p>Because Mormons believe that everyone must receive certain ordinances to be saved, Mormons perform ordinances on behalf of deceased persons.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> These ordinances are performed vicariously or by "proxy" on behalf of the dead. In accordance with their belief in each individual's "free agency", living or dead, Mormons believe that the deceased may accept or reject the offered ordinance in the <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Spirit world (Latter Day Saints)">spirit world</a>, just as all spirits decided to accept or reject God's plan originally. In addition, these "conditional" ordinances on behalf of the dead are performed only when a deceased person's genealogical information has been submitted to a temple and correctly processed there before the ordinance ritual is performed. Only ordinances for salvation are performed on behalf of deceased persons. <span class="nowrap"><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Baptism_for_the_dead" title="Baptism for the dead">Baptism for the dead</a>.</i></span>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scripture">Scripture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg/180px-Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg" decoding="async" width="180" height="323" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg/270px-Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg/360px-Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="2264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Latter-day_Saint_Scripture_Quadruple_Combination.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The LDS Church scriptural <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">canon</a></div></div></div>
<p>Mormons believe in the Old and New Testaments, and the LDS Church uses the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">King James Bible</a> as its official scriptural text of the <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Bible</a>. While Mormons believe in the general accuracy of the modern day text of the Bible, they also believe that it is incomplete and that errors have been introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In Mormon theology, many lost truths are restored in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a>, which Mormons hold to be divine scripture and equal in authority to the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mormon scriptural canon also includes a collection of revelations and writings contained in the <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_and_Covenants" title="Doctrine and Covenants">Doctrine and Covenants</a> which contains doctrine and prophecy and the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_of_Great_Price_(Mormonism)" title="Pearl of Great Price (Mormonism)">Pearl of Great Price</a> which addresses briefly Genesis to Exodus. These books, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith_Translation_of_the_Bible" title="Joseph Smith Translation of the Bible">Joseph Smith Translation of the Bible</a>, have varying degrees of acceptance as divine scripture among different denominations of the Latter Day Saint movement.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revelation">Revelation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Revelation_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Revelation (Latter Day Saints)">Revelation (Latter Day Saints)</a></div>
<p>In Mormonism, <a href="/wiki/Continuous_revelation" title="Continuous revelation">continuous revelation</a> is the principle that God or his divine agents still continue to communicate to mankind. This communication can be manifest in many ways: influences of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Ghost" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Ghost">Holy Ghost</a> (the principal form in which this principle is manifest), visions, visitations of divine beings, and others. Joseph Smith used the example of the Lord's revelations to Moses in <a href="/wiki/Deuteronomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy</a> to explain the importance of continuous revelation.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>God said, 'Thou shalt not murder' at another time He said, 'Thou shalt utterly destroy.' This is the principle on which the government of heaven is conducted–by revelation adapted to the circumstances in which the children of the kingdom are placed. Whatever God commands is right, no matter what it is, although we may not see the reason thereof till long after the events transpire.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Teachings_of_the_Prophet_Joseph_Smith_(book)" title="Teachings of the Prophet Joseph Smith (book)">Teachings of the Prophet Joseph Smith</a></i>, pp.&#160;256–257.</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Mormons believe that Smith and subsequent church leaders could speak scripture "when moved upon by the Holy Ghost."<sup id="cite_ref-personalrev_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-personalrev-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In addition, many Mormons believe that ancient prophets in other regions of the world received revelations that resulted in additional scriptures that have been lost and may, one day, be forthcoming. In Mormonism, revelation is not limited to church members. For instance, Latter Day Saints believe that the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Constitution">United States Constitution</a> is a divinely inspired document.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mormons are encouraged to develop a personal relationship with the Holy Ghost and receive personal revelation for their own direction and that of their family.<sup id="cite_ref-personalrev_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-personalrev-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The Latter Day Saint concept of revelation includes the belief that revelation from God is available to all those who earnestly seek it with the intent of doing good. It also teaches that everyone is entitled to <i>personal</i> revelation with respect to his or her <a href="/wiki/Stewardship_(theology)" title="Stewardship (theology)">stewardship</a> (leadership responsibility). Thus, parents may receive inspiration from God in raising their families, individuals can receive divine inspiration to help them meet personal challenges, church officers may receive revelation for those whom they serve.
</p><p>The important consequence of this is that each person may receive confirmation that particular doctrines taught by a prophet are true, as well as gain divine insight in using those truths for their own benefit and eternal progress. In the church, personal revelation is expected and encouraged, and many converts believe that personal revelation from God was instrumental in their conversion.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_to_other_faiths">Relations to other faiths</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_mainstream_Christianity">Relation to mainstream Christianity</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mormonism_and_Christianity" title="Mormonism and Christianity">Mormonism and Christianity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg/220px-Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg/330px-Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg/440px-Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1067" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Christus_statue_temple_square_salt_lake_city.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mormons see <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Christianity" title="Jesus in Christianity">Jesus Christ</a></span> as the premier figure of their religion.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Mormonism categorizes itself within <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, and nearly all Mormons self-identify as <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-aremormonschristian_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aremormonschristian-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> For some who define Christianity within the doctrines of <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, Mormonism's differences place it outside the umbrella of Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since its beginnings, the faith has proclaimed itself to be Christ's Church <a href="/wiki/Restorationism_(Christian_primitivism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Restorationism (Christian primitivism)">restored</a> with its original authority, structure and power; maintaining that existing denominations believed in incorrect doctrines and were not acknowledged by God as his church and kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Though the religion quickly gained a large following of Christian seekers, in the 1830s, many American Christians came to view the church's early doctrines and practices<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> as politically and culturally <a href="/wiki/Subversion_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Subversion (politics)">subversive</a>, as well as doctrinally heretical, abominable, and condemnable. This discord led to a series of sometimes-deadly conflicts between Mormons and others who saw themselves as orthodox Christians.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Although such violence declined during the twentieth century, the religion's unique doctrinal views and practices still generate criticism, sometimes vehemently so. This gives rise to efforts by Mormons and opposing types of Christians to proselytize each other.
</p><p>Mormons believe in Jesus Christ as the literal <a href="/wiki/Son_of_God_(Christianity)" title="Son of God (Christianity)">Son of God</a> and <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">Messiah</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion_of_Jesus" title="Crucifixion of Jesus">crucifixion</a> as a conclusion of a <a href="/wiki/Sin_offering" title="Sin offering">sin offering</a>, and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">resurrection</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> However, Latter-day Saints (LDS) reject the ecumenical creeds and the definition of the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> (In contrast, the second largest Latter Day Saint denomination, the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Christ" title="Community of Christ">Community of Christ</a>, is Trinitarian and <a href="/wiki/Monotheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Monotheistic">monotheistic</a>.) Mormons hold the view that the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> prophesied both the <a href="/wiki/Great_Apostasy" title="Great Apostasy">apostasy</a> from the teachings of Christ and his apostles as well as the <a href="/wiki/Restorationism_(Christian_primitivism)" class="mw-redirect" title="Restorationism (Christian primitivism)">restoration</a> of all things prior to the second coming of Christ.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some notable differences with mainstream Christianity include: A belief that Jesus began his <a href="/wiki/Substitutionary_atonement" title="Substitutionary atonement">atonement</a> in the garden of <a href="/wiki/Gethsemane" title="Gethsemane">Gethsemane</a> and continued it to his crucifixion, rather than the orthodox belief that the crucifixion alone was the physical atonement;<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> and an afterlife with three <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_glory" title="Degrees of glory">degrees of glory</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hell</a> (often called <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Latter_Day_Saints)#Spirit_prison" title="Spirit world (Latter Day Saints)">spirit prison</a>) being a temporary repository for the wicked between death and the resurrection.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, Mormons do not believe in creation <i><a href="/wiki/Ex_nihilo" title="Ex nihilo">ex nihilo</a></i>, believing that matter is eternal, and creation involved God organizing existing matter.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Much of the Mormon belief system is geographically oriented around the <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South American</a> continents. Mormons believe that the people of the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">Book of Mormon</a> lived in the <a href="/wiki/Western_hemisphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Western hemisphere">western hemisphere</a>, that Christ appeared in the western hemisphere after his death and resurrection, that the true faith was restored in <a href="/wiki/Upstate_New_York" title="Upstate New York">Upstate New York</a> by Joseph Smith, that the <a href="/wiki/Garden_of_Eden" title="Garden of Eden">Garden of Eden</a> was located in North America, and that the <a href="/wiki/New_Jerusalem" title="New Jerusalem">New Jerusalem</a> would be built in <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>. For this and other reasons, including a belief by many Mormons in <a href="/wiki/American_exceptionalism" title="American exceptionalism">American exceptionalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molly_Worthen" title="Molly Worthen">Molly Worthen</a> speculates that this may be why <a href="/wiki/Leo_Tolstoy" title="Leo Tolstoy">Leo Tolstoy</a> described Mormonism as the "quintessential 'American religion'".<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_Judaism">Relation to Judaism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mormonism_and_Judaism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormonism and Judaism">Mormonism and Judaism</a></div>
<p>Although Mormons do not claim to be part of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, Mormon theology claims to situate Mormonism within the context of Judaism to an extent that goes beyond what most other Christian denominations claim. The faith incorporates many <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> ideas into its theology, and the beliefs of Mormons sometimes parallel those of Judaism and certain elements of Jewish culture. In the earliest days of Mormonism, Joseph Smith taught that the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Indigenous peoples of the Americas</a> were members of some of the <a href="/wiki/Lost_Tribes_of_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Lost Tribes of Israel">Lost Tribes of Israel</a>. Later, he taught that Mormons were Israelites, and that they may learn of their tribal affiliation within the twelve Israelite tribes. Members of the LDS Church receive <a href="/wiki/Patriarchal_blessing" title="Patriarchal blessing">Patriarchal blessings</a> which declare the recipient's lineage within one of the tribes of Israel. The lineage is either through true blood-line or adoption. The LDS Church teaches that if one is not a direct descendant of one of the twelve tribes, upon baptism he or she is adopted into one of the tribes. Patriarchal blessings also include personal information which is revealed through a patriarch by the power of the priesthood.
</p><p>The Mormon affinity for Judaism is expressed by the many references to Judaism in the Mormon liturgy. For example, Smith named the largest Mormon settlement he founded <i><a href="/wiki/Nauvoo,_Illinois" title="Nauvoo, Illinois">Nauvoo</a></i>, which means "to be beautiful" in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a>. Brigham Young named a tributary of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Salt_Lake" title="Great Salt Lake">Great Salt Lake</a> the "<a href="/wiki/Jordan_River_(Utah)" title="Jordan River (Utah)">Jordan River</a>". The LDS Church created a writing scheme called the <a href="/wiki/Deseret_Alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Deseret Alphabet">Deseret Alphabet</a>, which was based, in part, on Hebrew. The LDS Church has a <a href="/wiki/BYU_Jerusalem_Center" title="BYU Jerusalem Center">Jerusalem Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, where students focus their study on Near Eastern history, culture, language, and the Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There has been some controversy involving Jewish groups who see the actions of some elements of Mormonism as offensive. In the 1990s, Jewish groups vocally opposed the LDS practice of <a href="/wiki/Baptism_for_the_dead" title="Baptism for the dead">baptism for the dead</a> on behalf of Jewish victims of <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> and Jews in general. According to LDS Church <a href="/wiki/General_authority" title="General authority">general authority</a> Monte J. Brough, "Mormons who baptized 380,000 Holocaust victims posthumously were motivated by love and compassion and did not understand their gesture might offend Jews ... they did not realize that what they intended as a 'Christian act of service' was 'misguided and insensitive'".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Mormons believe that when the dead are baptized through proxy, they have the option of accepting or rejecting the ordinance.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_Islam">Relation to Islam</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mormonism_and_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormonism and Islam">Mormonism and Islam</a></div>
<p>Since its origins in the 19th century, Mormonism has been compared to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, often by detractors of one religion or the other.<sup id="cite_ref-mohammed_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohammed-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> For instance, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Smith" title="Joseph Smith">Joseph Smith</a> was referred to as "the modern <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">mahomet</a>" &#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93; by the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald" title="New York Herald">New York Herald</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> shortly after his <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Joseph_Smith,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Death of Joseph Smith, Jr.">murder</a> in June 1844. This epithet repeated a comparison that had been made from Smith's earliest career,<sup id="cite_ref-mohammed_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohammed-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> one that was not intended at the time to be complimentary. Comparison of the Mormon and Muslim prophets still occurs today, sometimes for derogatory or <a href="/wiki/Polemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Polemical">polemical</a> reasons<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> but also for more scholarly (and neutral) purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-mohammed_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mohammed-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> While Mormonism and Islam have many similarities, there are also significant, fundamental differences between the two religions. Mormon–Muslim relations have been historically cordial;<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> recent years have seen increasing dialogue between adherents of the two faiths, and cooperation in <a href="/wiki/Charity_(practice)" title="Charity (practice)">charitable endeavors</a>, especially in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Islam and Mormonism both originate in the <a href="/wiki/Abrahamic_religions" title="Abrahamic religions">Abrahamic traditions</a>. Each religion sees its founder (Muhammad for Islam, and Joseph Smith for Mormonism) as being a true <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> of God, called to re-establish the truths of these ancient theological belief systems that have been altered, corrupted, or lost. In addition, both prophets received visits from an angel, leading to additional books of scripture. Both religions share a high emphasis on family life, charitable giving, chastity, abstention from alcohol, and a special reverence for, though not worship of, their founding prophet. Before the <a href="/wiki/1890_Manifesto" title="1890 Manifesto">1890 Manifesto</a> against plural marriage, Mormonism and Islam also shared in the belief in and practice of plural marriage, a practice now held in common by Islam and various branches of Mormon fundamentalism.
</p><p>The religions differ significantly in their views on God. Islam insists upon the complete <a href="/wiki/Tawheed" class="mw-redirect" title="Tawheed">oneness</a> and uniqueness of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a> (<a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>), while Mormonism asserts that the <a href="/wiki/Godhead_(Latter_Day_Saints)" class="mw-redirect" title="Godhead (Latter Day Saints)">Godhead</a> is made up of three distinct "personages."<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
Mormonism sees Jesus Christ as the promised Messiah and the literal Son of God, while Islam insists that the title "Messiah" means that Jesus (or "<a href="/wiki/Jesus_in_Islam" title="Jesus in Islam">Isa</a>") was a prophet sent to establish the true faith, not that he was the Son of God or a divine being. Despite opposition from other <a href="/wiki/Christian_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian denominations">Christian denominations</a>, Mormonism identifies itself as a Christian religion, the "restoration" of primitive Christianity. Islam does not refer to itself as "Christian", asserting that Jesus and all true followers of Christ's teachings were (and are) Muslims–a term that means <i>submitters to God</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Islam proclaims that its <a href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets of Islam">prophet</a> Muhammad was the "seal of the prophets",<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> and that no further prophets would come after him. Mormons, though honoring Joseph Smith as the first prophet in modern times, see him as just one in a long line of prophets, with Jesus Christ being the premier figure of the religion.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_divisions">Theological divisions</span></h2>
<p>Mormon theology includes three main movements. By far the largest of these is "mainstream Mormonism", defined by the leadership of <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (LDS Church). The two broad movements outside mainstream Mormonism are <a href="/wiki/Mormon_fundamentalism" title="Mormon fundamentalism">Mormon fundamentalism</a>, and liberal reformist Mormonism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream_Mormon_theology">Mainstream Mormon theology</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Beliefs_and_practices_of_The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="Beliefs and practices of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">Beliefs and practices of The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a></div>
<p>Mainstream Mormonism is defined by the leadership of the LDS Church which identifies itself as Christian.<sup id="cite_ref-aremormonschristian_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aremormonschristian-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Members of the LDS Church consider their top leaders to be prophets and <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Apostle (Latter Day Saints)">apostles</a>, and are encouraged to accept their positions on matters of theology, while seeking confirmation of them through personal study of the Book of Mormon and the Bible. Personal prayer is encouraged as well. The LDS Church is by far the largest branch of Mormonism. It has continuously existed since the <a href="/wiki/Succession_crisis_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Succession crisis (Latter Day Saints)">succession crisis</a> of 1844 that split the Latter Day Saint movement after the death of founder Joseph Smith, Jr.
</p><p>The LDS Church seeks to distance itself from other branches of Mormonism, particularly those that practice polygamy.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>
The church maintains a degree of orthodoxy by excommunicating or disciplining its members who take positions or engage in practices viewed as apostasy. For example, the LDS Church excommunicates members who practice polygamy or who adopt the beliefs and practices of Mormon fundamentalism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mormon_fundamentalism">Mormon fundamentalism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mormon_fundamentalism" title="Mormon fundamentalism">Mormon fundamentalism</a></div>
<p>One way Mormon fundamentalism distinguishes itself from mainstream Mormonism is through the practice of <a href="/wiki/Plural_marriage" class="mw-redirect" title="Plural marriage">plural marriage</a>. Fundamentalists initially broke from the LDS Church after that doctrine was discontinued around the beginning of the 20th century. Mormon fundamentalism teaches that plural marriage is a requirement for <a href="/wiki/Exaltation_(Latter_Day_Saints)" class="mw-redirect" title="Exaltation (Latter Day Saints)">exaltation</a> (the highest degree of salvation), which will allow them to live as gods and goddesses in the afterlife. Mainstream Mormons, by contrast, believe that a single <a href="/wiki/Celestial_marriage" title="Celestial marriage">Celestial marriage</a> is necessary for exaltation.
</p><p>In distinction with the LDS Church, Mormon fundamentalists also often believe in a number of other doctrines taught and practiced by <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young" title="Brigham Young">Brigham Young</a> in the 19th century, which the LDS Church has either abandoned, repudiated, or put in abeyance. These include:
</p>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_consecration" title="Law of consecration">law of consecration</a> also known as the <a href="/wiki/United_Order" title="United Order">United Order</a> (put in abeyance by the LDS Church in the 19th century);</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Adam%E2%80%93God_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Adam–God theory">Adam–God teachings</a> taught by Brigham Young and other early leaders of the LDS Church (repudiated by the LDS Church in the mid-20th century);</li>
<li>the principle of <a href="/wiki/Blood_atonement" title="Blood atonement">blood atonement</a> (repudiated by the LDS Church in the mid-20th century); and</li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Black_people_and_Mormonism" title="Black people and Mormonism">exclusion of black men</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Priesthood_(Latter_Day_Saints)" title="Priesthood (Latter Day Saints)">priesthood</a> (abandoned by the LDS Church in 1978).</li></ul>
<p>Mormon fundamentalists believe that these principles were wrongly abandoned or changed by the LDS Church, in large part due to the desire of its leadership and members to assimilate into mainstream American society and avoid the persecutions and conflict that had characterized the church throughout its early years. Others believe that it was a necessity at some point for "a restoration of all things" to be a truly restored Church.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_reformist_theology">Liberal reformist theology</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bickertonite" class="mw-redirect" title="Bickertonite">Bickertonite</a></div>
<p>Some LDS Church members have worked towards a more liberal reform of the church. Others have left the LDS Church and still consider themselves to be <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Mormon" class="mw-redirect" title="Cultural Mormon">cultural Mormons</a>. Others have formed new religions (many of them now defunct). For instance the <a href="/wiki/Godbeites" title="Godbeites">Godbeites</a> broke away from the LDS Church in the late 19th century, on the basis of both political and religious liberalism, and in 1985 the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_Church_of_Jesus_Christ" title="Restoration Church of Jesus Christ">Restoration Church of Jesus Christ</a> broke away from the LDS Church as an <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a>-friendly denomination, which was formally dissolved in 2010.
</p>

<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Standard language references such as Peter T. Daniels and William Bright, eds., <i>The World's Writing Systems</i> (New York: Oxford University Press, 1996) (990 pages); David Crystal, <i>The Cambridge Encyclopedia of Language</i> (Cambridge University Press, 1997); and Roger D. Woodard, ed., <i>The Cambridge Encyclopedia of the World's Ancient Languages</i> (Cambridge University Press, 2004) (1162 pages) contain no reference to "reformed Egyptian." "Reformed Egyptian" is also ignored in Andrew Robinson, <i>Lost Languages: The Enigma of the World's Undeciphered Scripts</i> (New York: McGraw Hill, 2002), although it is mentioned in Stephen Williams, <i>Fantastic Archaeology: The Wild Side of North American Prehistory</i> (Philadelphia: University of Pennsylvania Press, 1991). On their website, <i>Bad Archaeology</i>, two British archaeologists, Keith Fitzpatrick-Matthews and Dames Doeser, say "The only writing systems to have been recognized in the Americas are those used by the Maya and the Aztecs, neither of which resembles Egyptian hieroglyphs, although Joseph Smith produced a scrap of papyrus containing hieroglyphs he claimed to be a Reformed Egyptian text written by the Patriarch Abraham." <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.badarchaeology.com/lost-civilisations/the-lost-tribes-of-israel/">Bad Archaeology</a></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-44">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See King Follett discourse and <a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, p.&#160;73)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the Book of Moses, Lucifer offered an alternate plan that would guarantee the salvation of <i>all</i> spirits, however, at the cost of their agency, essentially forcing them to be saved. God's plan allowed spirits the freedom of choice but left room for some to fall out of his presence into darkness. <a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, p.&#160;73)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-46">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, p.&#160;77)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Nineteenth century Mormonism defined itself against Calvinistic religions that asserted humans' incapacity and utter dependence on the grace of God. Early Mormon preachers emphasized good works and moral obligation; however in the late twentieth century, Mormons pulled back from an "entrenched aversion" to the doctrines of grace, and today have an attitude of trusting in the grace of Christ while trying their best to do good works. <a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, p.&#160;76)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-48"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-48">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An ordinance is generally a physical act signifying a spiritual commitment or a covenant. Failure to honor that commitment results in the ordinance having no effect. However, sincere repentance can restore the blessings associated with the ordinance.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.churchofjesuschrist.org/study/manual/true-to-the-faith/ordinances?lang=eng">"Ordinances</a>," <i>True to the Faith</i>, (LDS Church, 2004) p. 109.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, pp.&#160;60–61)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Mormonism" title="Encyclopedia of Mormonism">Encyclopedia of Mormonism</a></i>, Macmillan 1992, pp. 106-107</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Matthews, Robert J., <i>A Bible! A Bible</i>, Bookcraft, 1990, p. 13</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, p.&#160;8) ("As the name of the church&#160;... suggests, Jesus Christ is the premier figure. <li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="/wiki/Bruce_McConkie" class="mw-redirect" title="Bruce McConkie">Bruce McConkie</a>, a <a href="/wiki/General_authority" title="General authority">general authority</a> of the LDS Church, "Mormonism is indistinguishable from Christianity." <cite id="CITEREFBruce_R._McConkie" class="citation">Bruce R. McConkie, <a href="/wiki/Mormon_Doctrine_(book)" title="Mormon Doctrine (book)"><i>Mormon Doctrine</i></a>, p.&#160;513</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Mormon+Doctrine&amp;rft.pages=513&amp;rft.au=Bruce+R.+McConkie&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMormonism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, a 2007 poll conducted by the Pew Research Center found that one in three Americans surveyed do not consider Mormons to be Christian. See for example <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.religionnewsblog.com/19478/religion-poll-2">ReligionNewsblog.com</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It is sometimes said that Mormonism is to Christianity as Christianity is to Judaism. Both Mormonism and Christianity established themselves by reinterpreting a preceding faith. Christianity built on Judaism but emphasized the death and resurrection of Jesus Christ; Mormonism began with Christianity but accepted new revelation through a modern prophet." <a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, p.&#160;62)</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-73"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-73">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFMcConkie1985" class="citation">McConkie, Bruce R. (May 1985), <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.churchofjesuschrist.org/study/ensign/1985/05/the-purifying-power-of-gethsemane?lang=eng">"The Purifying Power of Gethsemane"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ensign_(LDS_magazine)" title="Ensign (LDS magazine)">Ensign</a></i>: 9</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Ensign&amp;rft.atitle=The+Purifying+Power+of+Gethsemane&amp;rft.pages=9&amp;rft.date=1985-05&amp;rft.aulast=McConkie&amp;rft.aufirst=Bruce+R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.churchofjesuschrist.org%2Fstudy%2Fensign%2F1985%2F05%2Fthe-purifying-power-of-gethsemane%3Flang%3Deng&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMormonism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-74"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-74">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mormon scriptures speak of hell in two ways. The first is another name for <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Latter_Day_Saints)#Spirit_prison" title="Spirit world (Latter Day Saints)">spirit prison</a>, a place for the spirits of people who have "died in their sins." The second is a more permanent place called <a href="/wiki/Outer_Darkness" class="mw-redirect" title="Outer Darkness">Outer Darkness</a>, reserved for the Devil, his angels, and those who have committed the unpardonable sin. <i>True to the Faith: A Gospel Reference</i>, 2004, LDS Church. "<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://library.lds.org/nxt/gateway.dll/Curriculum/home%20and%20family.htm/true%20to%20the%20faith%20a%20gospel%20reference.htm/hell.htm?f=templates$fn=document-frame.htm$3.0#JD_36863Hel">Hell</a> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20100616000140/http://library.lds.org/nxt/gateway.dll/Curriculum/home%20and%20family.htm/true%20to%20the%20faith%20a%20gospel%20reference.htm/hell.htm?f=templates$fn=document-frame.htm$3.0#JD_36863Hel">Archived</a> 2010-06-16 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>," p. 81; See also: <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_Hell#Latter-day_Saints" title="Christian views on Hell">Christian views on Hell (Latter-day Saints)</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFBushman2008">Bushman (2008</a>, p.&#160;71)</span>
</li>
